{"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_01XgFNpkEA5ThMBo6fsgG1G5","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"thinking","thinking":"So the \"NFS reply getattr: -512\" is logged right after `rpc_call_sync()` in `nfs_proc_getattr()`. This means the `rpc_call_sync` returned -512 (ERESTARTSYS). The server did reply (tcpdump shows), but the client is returning ERESTARTSYS.\n\nThe key question is: why does `rpc_call_sync` return ERESTARTSYS when the server replied without error?\n\nLooking at the sched.c code:\n```c\nstatus = out_of_line_wait_on_bit(&task->tk_runstate,\n        RPC_TASK_QUEUED, rpc_wait_bit_killable,\n        TASK_KILLABLE);\nif (status == -ERESTARTSYS) {\n    dprintk(\"RPC: %5u got signal\\n\", task->tk_pid);\n    task->tk_flags |= RPC_TASK_KILLED;\n    rpc_exit(task, -ERESTARTSYS);\n}\n```\n\nThe task is sleeping in `TASK_KILLABLE` mode, so when a fatal signal arrives, `rpc_wait_bit_killable` returns -ERESTARTSYS and the task gets marked as killed and exits with that error. The issue is that the signal is interrupting the RPC call even though the server has already sent a valid response.\n\nI'm wondering if the problem is in how `out_of_line_wait_on_bit` behaves when the bit is already cleared before the wait even starts. Let me think about what changed between kernel 3.14 and 4.1 that could affect NFS or SunRPC signal handling. This is clearly a regression, so there must be something in the RPC scheduler or signal handling that changed. Given that this happens on a diskless system with NFS root and autofs on top, the issue likely involves signals being sent during autofs unmount operations that are now incorrectly interrupting RPC calls that should complete normally.\n\nThe tcpdump data is telling—the server sends the reply and the client receives it at the network level, but the kernel returns ERESTARTSYS instead. This points to a race where a fatal signal arrives while the RPC task is waiting, causing the task to exit before it can process the incoming reply. When there's a fatal signal, the task wakes up with ERESTARTSYS. But the key issue is: if the server reply has already been received AND there's also a fatal signal pending, which one wins?\n\nLooking at `__wait_on_bit`:\n```c\ndo {\n    prepare_to_wait(wq, &q->wait, mode);\n    if (test_bit(q->key.bit_nr, q->key.flags))\n        ret = (*action)(&q->key);\n} while (test_bit(q->key.bit_nr, q->key.flags) && !ret);\n\n\n```\n\nThe bit being checked is `RPC_TASK_QUEUED` in the task's `tk_runstate`. When the server reply arrives, `xprt_complete_rqst` clears this bit and wakes up the waiter. So in the loop: the task enters `TASK_KILLABLE` state, checks if the queued bit is still set, and if so calls the action function which checks for fatal signals.\n\nNow I'm tracing through what happens when the task actually gets woken up—the reply comes in and triggers the wake, but there's a race condition window where the task could be woken before it has a chance to run and check the bit again.\n\n```\nTask sleeps → Reply arrives and wakes it → Signal hits before task runs → Task checks fatal_signal_pending and returns -ERESTARTSYS → Loop exits with error even though the bit was cleared\n```\n\nSo the race is real: the task gets an error signal even though the reply already cleared the bit. {"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_01RBXXpcisdrMFtz7oAJcHHC","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"thinking","thinking":"So `fatal_signal_pending` checks for SIGKILL only. This is quite specific.\n\nSo for ERESTARTSYS to occur, SIGKILL must be pending for the process doing the NFS revalidation.\n\nNow, during autofs unmount, which process is doing the NFS revalidation? It's the automount daemon itself (or its worker threads). But the bug says the daemon continues to run. So it can't be SIGKILL sent to the daemon.\n\nWait, maybe the issue is different. Let me re-examine the ERESTARTSYS path. Looking at the sched.c code:\n\n\nThe RPC layer waits on a killable task state, and if it gets interrupted with ERESTARTSYS, it marks the task as killed and exits with that error status, which then propagates back through rpc_call_sync. But TASK_KILLABLE means the wait can be interrupted by signals...\n\nActually, looking at how signal_pending_state works, TASK_KILLABLE combines TASK_UNINTERRUPTIBLE with TASK_WAKEKILL, so even though it's not interruptible in the normal sense, the TASK_WAKEKILL flag allows fatal signals to wake it up and trigger the -ERESTARTSYS path.\n\nThe race condition hinges on timing: if the server reply arrives and clears the bit before SIGKILL is delivered, the task exits the wait normally and the signal becomes irrelevant. But if SIGKILL arrives first, it interrupts the wait and forces a restart.\n\nThe key insight is that `rpc_wait_bit_killable` checks for fatal signals at the start of each loop iteration—if SIGKILL is pending when the task wakes up, it returns -ERESTARTSYS immediately, regardless of whether the bit was actually cleared. I see the key issue now—in scenario 3, when SIGKILL wakes the task from TASK_KILLABLE sleep, the function returns 0 without checking the signal, so the loop continues and calls the action again, which finally detects the pending signal and returns -ERESTARTSYS. The race condition hinges on whether the signal arrives before or after the bit is cleared by the server's reply.\n\nThe real question is what changed in the wait_on_bit API between kernel versions—I'm thinking the action function signature or how it's invoked might have shifted around Linux 3.17, which could explain why the behavior differs between 3.14 and {"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_012WQwo5fAwATH3NvQcQ7rhP","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"thinking","thinking":"Now I can see the full `__rpc_execute` loop. When `out_of_line_wait_on_bit` returns -ERESTARTSYS, the code:\n1. Sets `task->tk_flags |= RPC_TASK_KILLED`\n2. Calls `rpc_exit(task, -ERESTARTSYS)` which sets `tk_status = -ERESTARTSYS` and `tk_action = rpc_exit_task`\n3. Continues the loop (doesn't break)\n4. Next iteration, `do_action = rpc_exit_task`, calls it\n\n\n5. `rpc_exit_task` clears `tk_action` and the loop breaks on the next iteration\n6. Returns with `tk_status = -ERESTARTSYS` all the way back to `rpc_call_sync`\n\nSo the real question is what changed in 3.14 with how `rpc_wait_bit_killable` handled signals and whether `freezable_schedule` was involved in the difference.\n\nActually, I should approach this differently—what's the actual regression between 3.14 and 4.1? I know there was a significant API change around kernel 3.17 or 3.18 where the `wait_on_bit` action function signature changed, and that would have affected how the RPC layer's wait mechanism worked. In 4.1, the implementation might have shifted to use `TASK_KILLABLE` instead of other states, which could explain the behavior difference. Let me look at what `out_of_line_wait_on_bit` is actually doing in both versions.\n\nBut wait—the tcpdump clearly shows the server sent a reply, yet the client returns -512 instead of receiving it. That's the real puzzle here. The reply is on the wire, but somehow it's not making it to the task that's waiting for it.\n\nSo the sequence must be: the getattr request goes out, a SIGKILL arrives while waiting, the task bails out with ERESTARTSYS before the reply lands, and then the server's response comes in but there's no task left to receive it—it's already been dequeued and cleaned up. The bug is that the kill signal causes the NFS client to abandon the request prematurely, even though the server is actively responding.\n\nNow I need to figure out where to fix this. The issue could be at several levels: maybe NFS operations shouldn't be killable at all, or maybe the problem is in how `nfs_lookup_revalidate` handles the ERESTARTSYS error—it's invalidating the dentry when it should just propagate the error instead. Or possibly the issue is deeper in `__nfs_revalidate_inode` itself, in how it handles the interrupted getattr.\n\nLooking at what actually should happen: when a process gets killed mid-operation, the dentry itself is still valid—it's just the process that's dying. So the dentry shouldn't be invalidated just because getattr was interrupted. This points to Option B being the right diagnosis: `nfs_lookup_revalidate` needs to stop invalidating the dentry on ERESTARTSYS and instead let the error propagate up.\n\nBut I should check what the actual kernel fix for this looks like, since this seems like a known issue. The solution likely involves returning a specific error code instead of invalidating the dentry when the operation gets interrupted.\n\nNow I'm wondering what actually changed between 3.14 and the later kernel version that caused this regression. In 3.14, the same SIGKILL scenario wouldn't cause the autofs mount to vanish, so either NFS getattr was using uninterruptible waits back then, or the error handling in `nfs_lookup_revalidate` was different, or something else entirely shifted in how these interruptions are handled.\n\nLet me approach this differently and look at what changed in the autofs module itself, since that's where the actual mount/unmount logic lives. The autofs daemon communicates through ioctls or pipes to trigger unmounts, but I'm wondering if there's something in the kernel autofs code that could be triggering a SIGKILL in a process context that wasn't happening before.\n\nActually, I need to reconsider the scenario more carefully. When autofs expires a mount and the daemon calls umount(), the kernel processes that unmount, and then /storage needs to be revalidated. The revalidation fails with ERESTARTSYS, but the key question is which process is doing the revalidating—is it the automount daemon itself, or another process that was accessing /storage when the unmount happened? If it's another process, they might receive signals during the unmount sequence, potentially including SIGKILL.\n\nWait, let me look at the bug report again. The automount daemon is logging an unmount error, which suggests the umount call itself failed. But the strange part is that /storage disappears even though only /storage/volume was supposed to expire. So what's happening is: /storage/volume unmounts successfully, but then /storage itself also gets unmounted. The autofs mount point /storage is disappearing because its dentry gets invalidated when nfs_lookup_revalidate returns 0, causing the VFS to invalidate the dentry.\n\nThe automount daemon is the one triggering this NFS revalidation when it calls umount(\"/storage/volume\"), which requires a path lookup through /storage. If SIGKILL is pending for the daemon at that moment, the NFS getattr could return ERESTARTSYS, invalidating /storage. But that seems unlikely. The bug occurs when the server's reply IS received (tcpdump shows it), but the client has already given up. This is a timing issue.Let me focus on the sunrpc sched.c and the specific change that would have caused this regression. I think the key is:\n\nIn `__rpc_execute`, when the sync task is sleeping and waiting for a reply:\n```c\nstatus = out_of_line_wait_on_bit(&task->tk_runstate,        RPC_TASK_QUEUED, rpc_wait_bit_killable,        TASK_KILLABLE);```\n\n\nThe task is in `TASK_KILLABLE`, which allows SIGKILL to interrupt it. I'm wondering if the 3.14 kernel used `TASK_UNINTERRUPTIBLE` instead, which would have prevented signal interruption and explains why this regression appeared. Let me trace through what `rpc_wait_bit_killable` does with different task states and check when this behavior changed.\n\nActually, I remember now that `rpc_wait_bit_killable` has been around since at least 3.10 and has always used `TASK_KILLABLE`, so that's not the source of the regression. I need to look elsewhere—maybe something in the autofs module or the kernel's shutdown sequence is triggering SIGKILL on these processes. Could there be a change in how NFS or the VFS layer handles autofs unmounting that causes processes to receive fatal signals? The issue was that `rpc_wait_bit_killable` would return `ERESTARTSYS` even when the RPC had already completed successfully, because it wasn't checking whether the wait condition had already been cleared before a signal arrived. The fix was to verify the bit state before returning the error, so a successfully completed RPC wouldn't be interrupted by a pending signal.\n\nBut looking more carefully at the actual code path, I'm realizing the signal handling happens in `call_allocate` when buffer allocation fails and there's a fatal signal pending. The confusing part is that the NFS debug output shows \"NFS reply getattr: -512\" which suggests the request was sent and a reply was received, but that log message gets printed regardless of whether the RPC actually succeeded or failed.\n\nSo the sequence is clearer now: the client sends the getattr request, then `rpc_call_sync` returns -512 (ERESTARTSYS) due to signal handling, and the client returns that error. The server's reply that tcpdump captured arrives later, but by then the task has already moved on. The real question is why SIGKILL is being delivered while the NFS operation is waiting, and whether there's something else going on besides the signal itself.\n\nLet me shift perspective and look at the sunrpc layer more carefully. I'm examining the `rpc_wait_bit_killable` function, which checks for fatal signals and returns ERESTARTSYS if one is pending, otherwise it schedules the task. This is the mechanism that's interrupting the wait, but I need to understand why the signal is arriving in the first place and whether there's been a change in how this is handled between kernel versions.\n\nThe issue I'm seeing is that if a fatal signal arrives while the task is queued waiting for a server reply, the function returns immediately without sleeping, which causes the wait loop to exit with ERESTARTSYS. But the task remains queued on the server side, so when the reply finally arrives, the client has already given up and returned an error to the application. This would perfectly explain why the server sends a valid response that the client never receives—the client has already abandoned the wait.\n\nThe fix could be to prevent `rpc_wait_bit_killable` from returning ERESTARTSYS if the reply is already pending, though that's difficult to check at that level. Alternatively, the higher-level code in `nfs_lookup_revalidate` needs to handle ERESTARTSYS differently instead of treating it as a definitive sign that the dentry is invalid. Let me look more closely at what `nfs_lookup_revalidate` does when it encounters this error and what specific change would resolve the regression.\n\nModifying the error handling logic... I'm narrowing down the bug to `fs/nfs/dir.c` in `nfs_lookup_revalidate`, where ERESTARTSYS from the RPC layer gets mishandled as a dentry invalidation rather than propagated as an error. The real question is whether the client is actually receiving the server's getattr reply but failing to process it correctly due to signal handling. The -512 return value is coming directly from `rpc_call_sync`, so the issue is likely in the RPC layer's handling of signals during synchronous calls. I'm narrowing it down to either `rpc_wait_bit_killable` in the scheduler where SIGKILL can interrupt the wait prematurely, or possibly in `nfs_lookup_revalidate` where the error handling might not be But that can't be right—if the buffer allocation failed, the request was never sent, so there wouldn't be a server reply to match the tcpdump evidence. The ERESTARTSYS must be coming from the `rpc_wait_bit_killable` path in sched.c instead, not from `call_allocate` in clnt.c. So the actual sequence is that buffer allocation succeeds first...\n\nNow I'm seeing the full picture: the request gets sent, the server replies, but while waiting for that reply a SIGKILL arrives and `rpc_wait_bit_killable` returns ERESTARTSYS, even though the reply is already in flight or has arrived. The bug is definitely in `net/sunrpc/sched.c` in the `rpc_wait_bit_killable` function called from `__rpc_execute`. The tricky part is deciding whether the fix belongs in sched.c or should be handled at the NFS layer instead.\n\nActually, the right approach is to fix this at the NFS level, not the RPC level, since the RPC behavior of returning ERESTARTSYS on signal is correct and expected. The issue is in `nfs_lookup_revalidate`: when `nfs_lookup_verify_inode` returns ERESTARTSYS, it currently goes to `out_bad` and returns 0 (marking the dentry invalid), but it should instead return -ERESTARTSYS to let the VFS handle the retry. When the VFS sees that negative error code, it propagates it back through the syscall layer so the operation can be properly restarted.\n\nThe real problem is that SIGKILL doesn't respect SA_RESTART—it just kills the process. But here's the thing: the autofs mount point is a kernel-level resource shared across all processes, not tied to any single one. If the dentry for /storage gets invalidated during the process's death, it affects every process that might try to access /storage later. So the fix really does need to be in `nfs_lookup_revalidate`—when inode revalidation fails with ERESTARTSYS, the dentry shouldn't be invalidated; the error should be propagated instead.\n\nWhen `nfs_lookup_revalidate` returns 0, the dentry becomes invalid and all mounts under /storage disappear. But if it returns -ERESTARTSYS, the VFS propagates the error to just that process's path lookup, leaving /storage valid for everyone else. So the solution is to modify the function to return -ERESTARTSYS rather than 0 when revalidation fails with that error.\n\nModifying the error handling {"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_01TMgm8Ln9JMEzoQcpcQTBZE","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"thinking","thinking":"Now I can see the full picture clearly!\n\nIn `nfs_lookup_revalidate`:\n1. When `nfs_lookup_verify_inode(inode, flags)` returns non-zero (e.g., -ERESTARTSYS), the code goes to `out_zap_parent` → `out_bad` → returns 0 (dentry invalid)\n2. There IS an `out_error` label that returns the actual error code (negative)\n3. But `nfs_lookup_verify_inode` returning an error doesn't go to `out_error` - it goes to `out_zap_parent`\n\n\n4. The fix is to check if the return value is actually an error code and route it to `out_error` instead, so signal interruptions like -ERESTARTSYS propagate correctly rather than being treated as invalid dentries. The issue is that when `nfs_lookup_verify_inode` returns a negative error like -ERESTARTSYS, returning 0 from d_revalidate tells the VFS the dentry is invalid, triggering a fresh lookup that unmounts everything below /storage. If d_revalidate returned the error instead, the VFS would propagate it to the caller without invalidating the dentry. This might be a regression from 3.14 to 4.1 where the error handling changed—perhaps the earlier version checked `< 0` instead of `!= 0`, or `nfs_lookup_verify_inode` didn't return ERESTARTSYS at all. I see the propagation path now—when `getattr` returns `-ERESTARTSYS`, it flows back through `__nfs_revalidate_inode` and then back to `nfs_lookup_revalidate`, which returns it directly. In 3.14, there might have been a signal-pending check that prevented calling `__nfs_revalidate_inode` in the first place, or the RPC layer handled signals differently.\n\nSince `TASK_KILLABLE` predates both versions, the real change must be elsewhere. I'm wondering if the verifier logic or how `nfs_lookup_verify_inode` handles signals changed between these kernel versions—maybe 4.x made it more aggressive about revalidating when signals are pending, whereas 3.14 had a guard that skipped revalidation in that case. The real issue might be that `nfs_check_verifier` changed its behavior between versions, causing `nfs_lookup_verify_inode` to execute more frequently. Or perhaps the regression is in `nfs_lookup_verify_inode` itself—it might now check for the `LOOKUP_OPEN` flag and perform an on-the-wire validation that it didn't do before, particularly for regular files.\n\nActually, I think the core problem is in `nfs_lookup_revalidate`: when `nfs_lookup_verify_inode` returns a negative error like `-ERESTARTSYS`, the code takes the wrong path and invalidates the dentry instead of propagating the actual error, which causes the `/storage` dentry to get zapped. The fix needs to route signal-related errors to `out_error` instead of continuing through the invalidation path. The primary bug is in `nfs_lookup_revalidate` around lines 1158-1162 where the error handling doesn't distinguish between transient RPC failures and actual validation failures, with a secondary issue in `rpc_wait_bit_killable` where ERESTARTSYS gets returned despite successful server When nfs_lookup_verify_inode() returns a negative error such as -ERESTARTSYS (-512), the code unconditionally jumps to out_zap_parent -> out_bad which returns 0 (dentry invalid). This causes the VFS to call d_invalidate() on the /storage dentry, unmounting the autofs mount. The fix should check whether the error is negative (a real error) and propagate it via the out_error label instead, so the dentry is NOT invalidated. The debug message 'NFS: nfs_lookup_revalidate(/storage) is invalid' at the out_bad label (returning 0) confirms this path. (2) Secondary: net/sunrpc/sched.c in rpc_wait_bit_killable. This function returns -ERESTARTSYS when fatal_signal_pending(current) is true, even before attempting to schedule. During autofs expiry/unmount, a fatal signal (SIGKILL) can be pending for the process performing the NFS getattr. The function immediately returns -ERESTARTSYS without waiting for the reply, which tcpdump confirms arrives later.
